MY HORROR CHAMBER
6
Preface
Preface
Many years ago when I was teaching a class in Functional Analysis at the Technical University of
Denmark, I was quite often challenged by my students who would ask: â€œIf we make the following
assumptions is it then possible to conclude some desired result not in the textbooks?â€ The answer was
usually â€œnoâ€, though sometimes the questions also struck some unexpected positive results. Anyway,
I always had to prepare a proof or counter-example for the following lecture. In this way a large
collection of strange examples in Mathematics grew up. One of my students, Eske Rahn, called this
collection The Horror Chamber of Mathematics. This explains the title of this book.
The purpose of this book is to show that Mathematics and even Calculus can be very diï¬ƒcult to
handle with lots of unexpected results, so one cannot just rely on some standardized exercises. I still
remember my ï¬rst encounter with such a strange example, which was not in my elementary books in
Mathematics. I had bought the newly published book of Riesz & Sz.â€“Nagy [47], in which I for the
ï¬rst time came across a singular function in the form of a continuous and strictly increasing function,
which is diï¬€erentiable â€œalmost everywhereâ€, and whenever it is diï¬€erentiable, its diï¬€erential quotient
is 0. In other words, if we ï¬rst diï¬€erentiate this function, and then take the Lebesque integral of the
result, we just get 0 and not the original function! This means that diï¬€erentiation and integration
are not always the inverse of each other. Such examples force the reader to think in an alternative
way instead of using the standard procedures. Without going into details, it can be mentioned that it
is the operation of diï¬€erentiation, which is the villain and not the Lebesque integral. Unfortunately,
the traditional teaching gives the impression that diï¬€erentiation is easy, while integration is an art,
to quote a professor from one of his lectures in the early 1960s. It should be added that the singular
functions were known long before the 1960s. The subject was not included in the usual elementary
courses.
Such weird examples can be used for inspiration of thinking in a new and diï¬€erent way. One example
is the clearly convergent integral

Ï€
2
0
dx
1 + tanÎ± x
for any ï¬xed Î± âˆˆR.
It is not diï¬ƒcult to calculate the integral for Î± = 0, 1, 2, and the result is in all cases the same, namely
Ï€/4. A head-on approach in the general case is doomed to fail, and computer programs like MAPLE
are also in trouble, though one gets a result as a decimal fraction. We need an idea, and the idea is

Elementary theory of functions
1
Elementary theory of functions
1.1
Introduction
In this chapter we give some examples which only involve â€œsimple functionsâ€. Some of them are
extremely elementary and they only show that one shall always be very meticulous when checking the
assumptions.
In the ï¬rst example I have deliberately â€œforgottenâ€ an assumption, which many readers will tacitly
assume to be fulï¬lled. We only use that a function can have relative minima everywhere without
being continuous. In the following example we also lack a crucial assumption.
The next two examples will probably be more surprising for the reader. In the ï¬rst example we con-
struct a function which is discontinuous everywhere in the set of rational numbers Q, and continuous
everywhere in the complementary set R \ Q of irrational numbers.
Then we construct a sequence of functions, which in some sense â€œconvergesâ€ towards Diracâ€™s Î´-
functionâ€, and which clearly does not converge pointwise towards Diracâ€™s Î´-functionâ€. This example
gives us a warning that the two natural deï¬nitions of convergence in fact are diï¬€erent, and they are
useful in diï¬€erent situations which may have nothing to do with each other. This shows that one
should always be very precise when we specify some deï¬nition, e.g. of convergence.
We then construct a continuous function, which is not diï¬€erentiable at any point. We shall later in
Chapter 2 meet some other bizarre examples of continuous functions, concerning their diï¬€erentiability
properties. However, the present example will for the time being be bad enough.
We continue discussing an hypothesis, which intuitively â€œmust be correctâ€. Then we demonstrate that
when we try to prove it, we really get into trouble! Therefore we go to plan B, which is to assume
the negation of the original hypothesis . . . and then again end up with another mathematical mess! In
this way we may continue with this mathematical ping-pong without getting any further, unless we
get a quite diï¬€erent idea. I shall of course for illustration ï¬nally solve the problem. Although only
Calculus known from high school is applied it is seen that the proof becomes far from obvious.
In the next example we must assume some elementary knowledge of Complex Functions Theory. Notice
that we in one of the constructions implicitly are using Banachâ€™s ï¬x point theorem. We learn from this
example that Taylor series are not always the best tool for approximation, which also follows from
one of the previous examples, where we constructed a continuous function, which is not diï¬€erentiable
at any point. In this case it does not make sense to talk about the corresponding Taylor series. It
simply does not exist. Instead we may use the classical and important WeierstraÃŸâ€™s approximation
theorem
Many years ago one of my friends asked me if some functional equation did only have one class of
solutions, depending on a single arbitrary constant. This would indeed be desirable from at statistical
point of view. Unfortunately, this hypothesis was wrong without some additional assumptions, so
the task became to ï¬nd such additional conditions, which assures that the functional equation indeed
had only the desired family of solutions. This has been included here as an illustrative example. The
project was quite a task, though not as bad as an exercise many years ago during my ï¬rst year of
study. My professor asked us to ï¬nd the error in some given hypothesis, then to correct the statement
and ï¬nally prove the right theorem. First year students! One would not challenge students today
with exercises of that sort.

The example above shows that it is possible to construct a function Ï• âˆˆCâˆ(R), such that
1) Ï•âˆ= 1, i.e. Ï• is in particular bounded.
2) Ï• is periodic.
3) The Taylor series from a point x0 âˆˆR is divergent for almost every x0 âˆˆR. And if the Taylor
series, expanded from some x0 âˆˆR is convergent, then it does not converge towards Ï•.
Another consequence is that a Taylor series expansion of a Câˆfunction does not always give us a
good approximation of the function under consideration. Fortunately, the WeierstraÃŸâ€™s approximation
theorem states that every continuous function (so we do not require that Ï• âˆˆCâˆ) in a closed and
bounded (i.e. compact) interval can be even uniformly approximated by a sequence of polynomials. If
we therefore in our calculations allow a uniform error < Îµ in a closed and bounded interval I, then
we can replace any continuous function in I by some polynomial.

1.10
A linear partial diï¬€erential equation without solution
Lewy [33] constructed in 1957 an extremely simple inhomogeneous partial diï¬€erential equation with
polynomial coeï¬ƒcients and where the right hand side is a Câˆfunction, such that this equation did
not have any solutions, not even in the sense of distributions, i.e. in the sense of generalized functions.
Later on, Lars HÂ¨ormander [27] found some suï¬ƒcient conditions for linear partial diï¬€erential operations
being of this strange type.
A simple twist of the present example gives us an injective diï¬€erential operator, which is hard to
believe, because one usually loses some information by diï¬€erentiation. We shall here in the large
follow Lewy [33].
His idea was to create a linear partial diï¬€erential operator, which turns every
function, or distribution, into an analytic function, so the range is a subset of the analytic functions.
If we then choose a Câˆfunction f, which is not analytic anywhere, cf. e.g. Section 1.8, as the right
hand side of the equation, then f does not lie in the range of the linear partial diï¬€erential operator,
so the equation does not have a solution.

Corollary 1.2 (HÂ¨ormander [27]) Given N â‰¥3, N âˆˆN.

diï¬€erential operator of ï¬rst order with Câˆcoeï¬ƒcients.

(1.43) does not have a solution. We claim that the only solution of the linear partial diï¬€erential
equation
(1.44)
âˆ‚u
âˆ‚x + i âˆ‚u
âˆ‚y âˆ’2i(x + iy = âˆ‚u
âˆ‚s + f Â· u = 0,
is the zero solution u = 0.
We shall assume that this is not the case, so we assume that there exists a solution u = 0 of (1.44).
There exists a branch of the complex logarithm and an open set Ï‰, such that v = ln u of class C1(Ï‰).
When we put u = ev into (1.44) and divide the result by ev = 0 in Ï‰, we get (1.43) with u replaced
by v, which contradicts that (1.43) does not have a solution. We conclude that the only solution of
(1.44) is u = 0, and since the operator is linear, it is also injective. â–¡
The nasty consequence of this example is that if we e.g. try so solve the equation
f â€²(x) = 2f(2x)
from Section 1.8 on a computer, then the approximation will always be an analytic function, i.e. in the
possible range of the diï¬€erential operator. If we therefore try to let the computer solve the problem,
it will most likely ï¬nd a â€œsolutionâ€. So the computer is trying to approximate the empty set! This is
an extreme example showing that one cannot always trust the results obtained on a computer.

Integrals
2.1
Some necessary measure theory
The present chapter is containing examples from Measure Theory, so we shall start with introducing
the most necessary concepts from this theory.
Most of them are intuitively clear, and yet their
consequences may sometimes seem very strange.
Only one example requires more than just the
knowledge of nullsets. The basics given here are also used in the Chapters 3, 6 and 8.
Apart from ï¬nite pointsets the simplest sets in R we can think of are the intervals, of which we have
four types,
[a, b] = {x âˆˆR | a â‰¤x â‰¤b}
for âˆ’âˆ< a â‰¤b < +âˆ,
(closed interval)
]a, b] = {x âˆˆR | a < x â‰¤b},
for âˆ’âˆâ‰¤a < b < +âˆ,
(halfopen interval)
[a, b[ = {x âˆˆR | a â‰¤x < b},
for âˆ’âˆ< a < b â‰¤+âˆ,
(halfopen interval)
]a, b[ = {x âˆˆR | a < x < b},
for âˆ’âˆâ‰¤a < b â‰¤+âˆ,
(open interval).
Whenever I is such an interval with ï¬nite a and b, the measure of I is deï¬ned as |I| = b âˆ’a, i.e. the
measure is the length of the interval, no matter whether the endpoints are included in I or not.
Based on the 1-dimensional intervals above we introduce in the N-dimensional real space RN,
Deï¬nition 2.1 An interval in RN, N âˆˆN, is a set of the form
I = I1 Ã— Â· Â· Â· Ã— IN,
where each Ij, j = 1, . . . , N, is an interval in R.
If each Ij, j = 1, . . . , N, is bounded with the measure |Ij| < +âˆ, we say that I = I1 Ã— Â· Â· Â· Ã— IN is a
bounded interval of the measure
|I| = |I1 Ã— Â· Â· Â· Ã— IN| = |I1| Â· Â· Â· |IN| .
Clearly, the bounded intervals in the plane R2 are rectangles, and the bounded intervals of the 3-space
R3 are axiparallel boxes. It follows immediately from Deï¬nition 2.1 that there are four diï¬€erent types
of intervals in R2, and in general 2N diï¬€erent intervals in RN, N âˆˆN. Whenever they are bounded,
the 2N diï¬€erent intervals based on the same box have all the same measure. Since the emphasis is put

Deï¬nition 2.4 We say that two functions f and g are equal almost everywhere, and we write
f = g
a.e.,
if the set

is a nullset.
If f and g are equal almost everywhere, and f is Lebesgue integrable, then so is g, and

g dx.
This means that the Lebesgue integral cannot distinguish between function which are equal almost
everywhere. It can be proved that â€œ= a.e.â€ is an equivalence relation, and if an element of one of
its equivalence classes is Lebesgue integrable, then all functions in this equivalence class are Lebesgue
integrable with the same value of the their Lebesgue integral.
Roughly speaking, one may say that we get the Lebesgue integrable functions by taking all Riemann
integrable functions and add to them all nullfunctions. This statement is not quite correct, â€œbut
almostâ€. This inaccuracy will, however, not be of any importance in the following, so in order to
just give a hint we mention that the standard example of a Riemann integrable function which by
deï¬nition is not Lebesgue integrable, is the function f(x) = sin(x)/x for x > 0.

2.2
Short overview of the contents of this chapter.
The ï¬rst three examples require only some knowledge of the Riemann integral. In Section 2.3 we
demonstrate that integration of a continuous function over a bounded, though not closed domain
in the plane may cause some problems. For the chosen function the two possible axiparallel double
integrals both exists, but they are not equal to each other.
We shall not go too deep into the
explanation, because that would involve the Lebesgue integral, and this is not the main subject here.
In Section 2.4 we construct a function, which is diï¬€erentiable everywhere in R, but its derivative is
not absolutely integrable over [0, 1]. This gives us the hint that the classical concept of diï¬€erentiation
may have some nasty properties, which at best should be avoided. Later on we shall see even worse
examples, both in the present chapter, as well as in Chapter 5.
In Section 2.5 we play a little with integration. We shall not yet apply the Lebesgue integral, although
the example is better explained in Lebesgue theory.
However, the basic calculations are easy to
understand even with only the Riemann integral at hand. The starting point is the following nonsense,
â€œone half indeï¬nite integration from 0â€. Once the meaning of this phrase has been understood (it
is called fractional integration), it is easy also to introduce â€œindeï¬nite integration Î± times from 0â€,
where Î± is any real, positive number, like e.g. âˆšÏ€, just to mention one of the more bizarre possible
numbers. We shall not go through all details in the general formula, because this would require some
knowledge of the Î“ and the B functions.
In spite of the above, fractional integration is not as exotic as one would think. It actually occurs
quite natural in some practical cases, including situations from the technical sciences. The earliest
implicit application of this theory can even be traced back to Abel [1], [2], in connection with the
problem of the tautochron.
We continue in Section 2.6 and Section 2.7 demonstrating that convergence in Lp is not in harmony
with the usual well-known pointwise convergence. This is a hidden warning! The inexperienced student
would always prefer to apply the pointwise convergence instead of the more useful convergence in Lp.
In practice, when one has made some measurements, the natural concept will mostly be L2, or L1, or
Lâˆ. These are used in the maximum likelihood method and in convergence in energy.
In Sections 2.8 and 2.9 we show that if p = q, then there are Lp functions which do not belong to Lq,
and vice versa.

3.1
Introduction
In Section 3.2 and Section 3.3 we show some classical examples, which should be included in every
collection of â€œstrangeâ€ examples.
Cf. also Gelbaum and Olmsted [22] and Mejlbo [35] (not to be
confused with the present author). These examples are also connected with the theory of fractals,
though they are much older.
The example of Section 3.4 was the ï¬rst one which many years ago aroused my attention to these
examples. I have not been able to ï¬nd the right reference, but it may probably be found in Riesz-Sz.
Nagy [47]. We construct a continuous and strictly increasing function, which is diï¬€erentiable almost
everywhere, and its derivative is 0, when it exists,
The Kakeya problem is the subject of Section 3.5. The task is to ï¬nd the Lebesgue measure of the
smallest subset â„¦of R2, such that an inï¬nitely thin needle of length 1 can be moved inside â„¦and
eventually come back to its original position, only with the needle pointing in the opposite direction.
The Nikodym set of Section 3.6 is closely connected with the Kakeya problem.
We show in Section 3.7 a counterexample from Complex Functions Theory. The example is put here,
because the methods used are more in line with the already mentioned examples than with analytic
functions.
Finally, we construct in Section 3.8 a metric space and two diï¬€erent probability measures, which
nevertheless are identical on every ball in this metric space.

3.5
The Kakeya problem
The Kakeya problem is also called the needle problem. The problem is to ï¬nd the inï¬mum of the
area |A| of all sets A âŠ†R2, which have the following property. An inï¬nitely thin needle of length 1
is moved around inside A in such a way that it can return to its original position, but in its reverse
sense, where its two endpoints are interchanged.
The problem was originally posed by Kakeya [30] in 1917. It was solved by Besicovitch [7] in 1928.
In the same year his construction was improved by Perron [44]. Later improvements were given in
1962 by Rademacher [46] and Schoenberg [48]. The presentation in the following is in broad outline
following Rademacher [46].

3.6
The Nikodym set
A Nikodym set is a set N âŠ‚[0, 1]2 with the following two properties,
1) Its measure is |N| = 1.
2) To every x âˆˆN we can ï¬nd an inï¬nite straight line â„“(x), such that N âˆ©â„“(x) = {x}.
.
Condition 2) means that every x âˆˆN can be reached by a full straight line lying in the complementary
set of N, with the exception of the point {x} itself. And yet condition 1) tells us that |N| = 1, so


xâˆˆN
â„“(x) \ N
 = 0.
The existence of such a set was ï¬rst shown by Nikodym [41] in 1927. His elementary, though extremely
complicated proof was later simpliï¬ed by Davies [17] and Cunninngham [15], [16].
The titles of
Cunninghamâ€™s two papers show that this problem is closely connected with the Kakeya problem of
Section 3.5.
The construction here is based on the following
Theorem 3.5 There exists a nullset K âŠ‚R2, such that to every x âˆˆR2 there exists an inï¬nite
straight line â„“(x), such that
x âˆˆâ„“(x)
and
â„“(x) âŠ‚K âˆª{x}.
So every point x âˆˆR2 lies on a straight line â„“(x), which is contained in the union of {x} and the
nullset K.
Once Theorem 3.5 has been proved, and K is a nullset with the properties of Theorem 3.5, we get
a Nikodym set by N = [0, 1]2 \ K. In fact, |N| = 1 is obvious, and if x âˆˆN, then it follows from
Theorem 3.5 that we can ï¬nd a straight line â„“(x) in the plane, such that
â„“(x) âˆ©B = {x}.
The proof of Theorem 3.5 relies on two lemmata, of which the ï¬rst one below is the hard one. The
proof is a purely classical geometric proof of a type which today is almost forgotten.

3.7
A set which is ï¬lling almost all of R without containing any homoge-
neous subset.
Some problems in Complex Functions Theory require that a given subset of R contains an homogeneous
subset in the sense of Carleson (to be deï¬ned below). It was e.g. proved in KahanpÂ¨aÂ¨a and Mejlbro [29]
the already well-known fact that the Hilbert transform of a function f is totally controlled (in the
Lp norm) over a set A âŠ†R, if and only if A is homogeneous. It was in the same paper proved that
to every Îµ > 0 there exists a closed set A, such that |R \ A| < Îµ, and such that no subset B âŠ†A is
homogeneous. We include here the construction of the set A of these properties, because it may have
interest in itself, once we have deï¬ned, what is meant by an homogeneous set.
Let us start with the deï¬nition of Carlesonâ€™s homogeneous set.
Deï¬nition 3.3 A nonempty measurable subset A âŠ†R is called homogeneous, if there exists a con-
stant c > 0, such that
(3.24)
|A âˆ©[x âˆ’r, x + r]| > 2cr = c|[x âˆ’r, x + r]|
for all x âˆˆA and all r > 0.
In other words, a nonempty measurable set A is homogeneous, if every bounded interval I with its
midpoint lying in A, contains some portion of A, at least the fraction c of the length of the interval I.
For some obscure reason, which is not clearly understood, it is the geometrical assumption that the
midpoint of I should lie in A, which makes this construction of the example below possible.
The deï¬nition means intuitively that no point in A is â€œsparseâ€ in A. If we let r â†’0+ in (3.24), then
roughly speaking the homogeneous set A is more or less uniformly distributed.
For convenience later on we get by a negation of Deï¬nition 3.3 that a nonempty subset A of R is not
homogeneous, if for every constant c > 0 we can ï¬nd x âˆˆA and r > 0, such that
(3.25)
|A âˆ©[x âˆ’r, x + r]| < 2cr.
In order to become familiar with homogeneous sets we include a couple of simpler examples.
Example 3.1 Every halï¬‚ine is homogeneous. It suï¬ƒces to prove this for the halï¬‚ine A = [0, +âˆ[.
We see that the measure |A âˆ©[x âˆ’r, x + r]| is smallest for x âˆˆA, if x = 0, in which case
|A âˆ©[âˆ’r, r] = |[0, r]| = r = 1
2 Â· 2r,
proving that the homogeneity constant is c = 1
2. â™¦

4.1.1
Overview of the contents of the chapter
We shall in this chapter give some classical examples from the Theory of Fourier Series. In the present
section we brieï¬‚y sketch the most necessary of the theory of Fourier series. In Section 4.2 we discuss
the Gibbsâ€™s phenomenon, which from time to time also occurs in the engineering literature. Then we
discuss in Section 4.3 the sequence of functions
sin nx
Ï€x

nâˆˆN

The next three sections are concerned with pointwise convergence of Fourier series. We construct
in Section 4.4 a continuous periodic function, which has a Fourier series which is divergent at some
points. We continue in Section 4.5 with Kolmogorovâ€™s example of a function from L1([0, 2Ï€[), the
Fourier series of which is pointwise divergent almost everywhere. Finally, we ï¬nish the discussion of
convergency in Section 4.6 by presenting Kolmogorovâ€™s example of a function from L1([0, 2Ï€[) which
has a Fourier series diverging pointwise everywhere.
The most successful application of the theory of Fourier series takes place in L2([0, 2Ï€[), which can be
considered as a Hilbert space with a countable orthogonal basis. Also, from a physical point of view,
a function from L2([0, 2Ï€[) represents in some sense an energy, so there is here a strong connection
between Physics and Mathematics.
In Section 4.7 we brieï¬‚y sketch the Besicovitch space, which is an example of a nonseparable Hilbert
space. Such spaces are usually not included in the elementary courses in Mathematics at the univer-
sities. The problem with them is that they have â€œtoo manyâ€ (non-countable) mutually orthonormal
unit vectors, However, from a philosophical point of view such spaces might give a far more realistic
model of reality than the ordinary Fourier series.
The theory of Fourier series is closely connected with the Lebesgue integral. It easily follows from
HÂ¨olderâ€™s inequality, cf. Section 2.1, that Lp([0, 2Ï€[) is contained in L1([0, 2Ï€[) for every p > 1.

Remark 4.3 It was a long time an open question, if it was possible to construct a continuous periodic
function f, where the corresponding Fourier series is divergent everywhere. This conjecture was partly
supported by the example above, and partly by Kolmogorovâ€™s examples, cf. Section 4.5 and Section 4.6.
Kolmogorovâ€™s examples do not solve the problem, because the functions are not continuous. However,
they are absolutely integrable, f âˆˆL1([0, 2Ï€[), and the condition  2Ï€
0
|f(t)| dt < +âˆguarantees that
all Fourier coeï¬ƒcients are well-deï¬ned.
The problem above was solved by Carleson [11] in 1966, and his result was extended in 1967 by
Hunt [26]. Summing up, they proved the following theorem
Carleson-Huntâ€™s theorem.
For every p > 1 and every function f âˆˆLp([0, 2Ï€[) there exists a
Lebesgue nullset A âŠ‚[0, 2Ï€[, such that
lim

for all x âˆˆ[0, 2Ï€[ \A.
In other words, this theorem assures for p > 1 that every f âˆˆLp([0, 2Ï€[) is pointwise convergent
almost everywhere. We notice that the function f given by (4.25) is continuous and periodic, so its
restriction to the interval [0, 2Ï€[ belongs to everyone of the spaces Lp([0, 2Ï€[), p â‰¥1. Then it follows
from Carleson-Huntâ€™s theorem that its Fourier series is pointwise convergent almost everywhere. It
was shear luck that the choice x = 0 actually belongs to the exceptional nullset. â™¦

We notice that Kolmogorovâ€™s two functions deï¬ned in Section 4.5 and Section 4.6 necessarily must

The interested reader may ï¬nd a proof of Carleson-Huntâ€™s theorem in JÃ¸rsboe & Mejlbro [28]. This is
not an easy proof either (121 pages), and even today I feel that the result in some way is not â€œnaturalâ€.
In all useful applications in the physical or technical sciences the Fourier series are convergent in
L2([0, 2Ï€[), i.e. in limit in mean, l.i.m. or in energy. It is in such a context a very strange request
that it should also be pointwise convergent, and even more strange that this is actually true almost
everywhere in most cases of practical interest.
It should be added that there exist continuous and periodic function, where Carleson-Huntâ€™s theorem
applies, and where the Fourier series diverges at all points from a non-countable set, which then
necessarily is a nullset. And even more general, to every nullset N âŠ‚[0, 2Ï€[ we can ï¬nd a continuous
function, such that its Fourier series is divergent at all points x âˆˆN.

4.7
Some â€œwildâ€ Hilbert spaces
In most introductory descriptions of Functional Analysis one only considers separable Hilbert spaces,
i.e. Hilbert spaces which contains a countable dense subset. The reason is that a Hilbert space has
a countable orthonormal basis, if and only if it is separable. The prototype of a separable Hilbert
space is L2([0, 2Ï€[), or equivalently â€“ and more convenient for our purposes in this section â€“ the space
L2([âˆ’Ï€, Ï€[) where the interval [âˆ’Ï€, Ï€[ has the center x = 0. We shall start with
4.7.1
Short review of the standard Hilbert space L2([âˆ’Ï€, Ï€[)
We shall for convenience assume that we are dealing with complex separable Hilbert spaces over the
ï¬eld of complex numbers C. It can be proved that there is essentially only one such separable Hilbert
space, because they are all mutually isomorphic. We can therefore restrict ourselves to the standard
example. Notice for the geometrical mind, that the Hilbert space in some sense is the generalisation
of the Euclidean spaces En of dimension n âˆˆN to inï¬nite dimension. We may therefore expect some
deï¬nitions which have a ring of Geometry, like e.g. inner product and orthogonality, and these concepts
are indeed essential in the theory of Hilbert spaces.
We shall in this section consider the standard example of a separable Hilbert space over C, namely
L2([âˆ’Ï€, Ï€[). Recall that an element f âˆˆL2([âˆ’Ï€, Ï€[) is a (measurable) function on [âˆ’Ï€, Ï€[), for which

Historical remarks
Obviously, if (Î»n)nâˆˆZ = (n)nâˆˆZ in (4.50), then we get the usual Fourier series.
The structure of these usual Fourier series inspired Harald Bohr [8], [9], [10], in 1923 to generalise
the Fourier series by allowing more general exponents of the exponential function eiÎ»t. In total Bohr
wrote 54 papers on this subject, where we here only refer to the ï¬rst three of them.
One must here realise that in 1923 Lebesgueâ€™s theory of integration from 1907 had not yet been applied
to form the Hilbert spaces, so these were not known to Bohr. His idea was instead to consider the
closed hull of all ï¬nite sums of the form

being too technical. The advantage of this choice is of course that everyone of Bohrâ€™s almost periodic
functions are bounded and continuous, because they can be written as a limit of a uniformly convergent
sequence of continuous functions of the form (4.51).
It is noteworthy to see that Bohr actually uses Hilbert space methods before the Hilbert space theory
was known. In fact, Bohr ï¬rst introduces the mean value operator,

5.1
Introduction
We shall in this chapter go through two classical examples of space ï¬lling curves. We consider in
Section 5.2 the famous Peanoâ€™s curve,, and in Section 5.3 we modify Peanoâ€™s curve to get Osgoodâ€™s
curve.. When we ï¬nally in Section 5.4 combine Peanoâ€™s curve with Cantorâ€™s function, introduced in
Section 3.3, we get a curve passing though every point of the unit square, i.e. its graph has positive
area 1, and yet this curve is â€œalmost everywhereâ€ (in the parameter domain) â€œalmost nowhereâ€ (in
the range, i.e. it lies almost nowhere in the unit square). In particular, it follows that nullsets are not
necessarily mapped into nullsets by a continuous map.
Obviously, we must reconsider what is meant by a continuous curve. From time to time one comes
across in the literature the following heuristic deï¬nition of a continuous curve,
Pseudo deï¬nition. â€œThe graph of a continuous curve is an inï¬nitely thin line, which can be drawn
without lifting the pencil from the paper.â€
This â€œdeï¬nitionâ€ is of course very convenient in most practical cases, because it contains the idea of a
curve. However, it implicit assumes that the curve is smooth in some sense and not just continuous.
E.g. piecewise diï¬€erentiable.
Once WeierstraÃŸ in 1861 constructed a continuous function, which
was not diï¬€erentiable anywhere, cf. Section 1.6, his result implicitly also implied that the pseudo
deï¬nition above was far too imprecise, though the mathematical society still did not react. The next
step forward in the direction of a reconsideration was given by Peano [43] in 1890, when he constructed
a continuous curve in the plane, which went through every point of the unit square, in which case the
pseudo deï¬nition above clearly does not apply.
We shall in Section 5.2 go through the classical construction of Peano without using the alternative
construction of David Hilbert.
Peanoâ€™s curve contained a lot of double points. Then in 1903 Osgood [42] modiï¬ed Peanoâ€™s construc-
tion and constructed a curve without double points, such that the area of its graph is â‰¥1 âˆ’Îµ, where
Îµ âˆˆ]0, 1[ is any given constant. The editor of Trans. Amer. Math. Soc. must in 1903 have thought
that this example was very extraordinary, because Osgoodâ€™s contribution was supplied with attached
ï¬gures in colour to describe his procedure. This must have been very expensive in those days. We
shall here not go that far.
Osgoodâ€™s curve is also classical stuï¬€, so we describe the construction in Section 5.3. This is a curve
without double points in the unit square, and yet its graph has an area â‰¥1 âˆ’Îµ, where Îµ âˆˆ]0, 1[ can
be chosen as small as we want.
Instead of the pseudo deï¬nition above we shall in the following use the more precise
Deï¬nition 5.1 Let I âŠ‚R be an interval, and let Ï• : I â†’R2 be a continuous map. We then call Ï• a
continuous curve in the plane.
We shall in the beginning also assume that I is bounded and closed, i.e. compact, because then it
follows from one of the main theorems of continuous functions that then the range Ï•(I) is compact,
i.e. bounded and closed.
We mention that if I is not bounded, or not closed, then Ï•(I) may be unbounded.

MY HORROR CHAMBER
173
Continuous curves
Theorem 5.2 There exists a continuous curve, which runs through every point of the unit square.
It is not hard to construct a generalised Peanoâ€™s curve which runs through all points of the plane. We
only have to allow the parameter interval to be R. That I previously was chosen bounded was only
due to some technicalities in the basic construction.
Figure 5.3: The ordering of all squares in the plane. Peanoâ€™s curve runs through a diagonal, so one
must check that the diagonals match each others at their endpoints. This is possible by the ordering
in the double spiral on the ï¬gure.
The basic Peano curve goes from one diagonal point to the opposite diagonal point. We shall just be
convinced that it is possible to glue together inï¬nitely many Peano curves to get an inï¬nite continuous
Peano curve, which runs through every point of the plane R2. The simplest possibility is shown on
Figure 5.3. The double spiral indicates the ordering of the subsquares, such that every endpoint of
one chosen diagonal of one subsquare is identical with the initial point of the chosen diagonal in the
next subsquare. This construction is obviously possible.
Another extension is to a space ï¬lling curve f : [0, 1] â†’[0, 1]3 in the 3-dimensional space R3. The
basic construction is easily illustrated by Rubikâ€™s cube. We consider the unit cube and start by moving
along the diagonal from the point (0, 0, 0) to the point (1, 1, 1). Then apply the 2-dimensional Peano
construction, described above with the only modiï¬cation that we instead always use the 3-dimensional
diagonal. So if a diagonal of a subcube starts from a point at the lower face of a layer of Rubikâ€™s
cube, then it will always end at the upper face of this layer, and vice versa. Since we move through an
odd number of subcubes, namely 9, and start at a point on the lower face, we shall end at the upper
face of the lowest layer of Rubikâ€™s cube. This is, however, the lower face of the middle layer, so the
process can be repeated, until we end at the upper face of the middle layer, which also is the lower
face of the upper layer, and the procedure is then obvious, because we just repeat this construction
of al subcubes of the next level of division, etc.. The proof itself is just a repetition of the previously
given concerning the plane Peano curve with obvious modiï¬cations.
It is left as an exercise to the reader to ï¬nd an ordering of all unit cubes in R3, such that we by this
construction obtain a continuous curve, which runs through all points of R3.

Continuous curves
The extension to a similar construction to general RN is almost obvious. It is also left to the reader.
An immediate consequence of Theorem 5.2 is that it is possible continuously to map a line segment
(of area zero) onto a set of positive area. One might here object that the parameter interval I = [0, 1]
has the 1-dimensional measure 1 in the space R, and that we are dealing with two diï¬€erent spaces.
Unfortunately, it is not so simple that images of continuous maps and of positive area stem from sets
of positive area in the parameter interval. A counterexample is given in Section 5.4, in which we
construct a curve, where a nullset of the parameter interval is mapped continuously onto a set of area
1. So measure is not preserved by continuous functions. We must add some extra conditions to obtain
such a desirable result.
As a preparation of Section 5.3 we repeat the fact that already the function f1 has double points,
thus also following functions fn, as well as the limit function f. In particular, f is not injective. It is
possible to prove that if the range f([0, 1]) of a continuous curve contains interior points, then f cannot
be injective. Notice that this is by no way contradicting the construction in Section 5.3. Although
Osgoodâ€™s curve has a positive area, it cannot have inner points, because it is injective, so its interior
is the empty set.

b) area(f([0, 1]) â‰¥1 âˆ’Îµ.

The curve constructed above is called Osgoodâ€™s curve. It can be used to illustrate the diï¬ƒculties of
the following classical theorem. We shall ï¬rst introduce some terminology. Every injective curve,
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

MY HORROR CHAMBER
177
Continuous curves
i.e. without double points, is called a simple curve. A Jordan curve is a simple curve, except for its
coinciding endpoints, i.e. a Jordan curve is a closed curve. Every Jordan curve in the plane divides
R2 into three mutually disjoint subsets, namely the Jordan curve itself, the open interior of the curve,
and the open exterior of the curve. The classical result is the following theorem,
Jordanâ€™s curve theorem. The complementary set of a Jordan curve in the plane has a bounded and
an unbounded connected component.
Figure 5.5: Sketch of a nontrivial Jordan curve of positive area. The four copies of Osgoodâ€™s curve
are indicated by squares with a diagonal to show the direction of the curve.
The complications of Jordanâ€™s curve theorem may be illustrated in the following way. If we combine
four copies of Osgoodâ€™s curve as indicated on Figure 5.5 and with the connecting line segments, we
obtain a Jordan curve of positive area contained in the unit square [0, 1]2 and of area 1 âˆ’Îµ, where
Îµ âˆˆ]0, 1[ can be any constant. One can easily imagine that the proof of Jordanâ€™s curve theorem must
be diï¬ƒcult, because how do we decide whether a point in [0, 1]2 not lying on the Jordan curve itself
is an interior or an exterior point? Notice that the Jordan curve is closed, so the complementary
set is open. In particular, the bounded component, i.e. the interior of the curve, is open, hence even
curve connected. This means that every two points inside the Jordan curve can be connected by a
continuous curve, which does not intersect the Jordan curve. When one considers the construction of
Osgoodâ€™s curve it does not look like an easy job to connect two such points in the interior.
As already mentioned above, another strange observation is that one can deï¬ne a Jordan curve in
[0, 1]2, such that its area is â‰¥1 âˆ’Îµ for any given Îµ âˆˆ]0, 1[. By a remark in Section 5.2 no injective
curve can contain interior points, so the interior of the Jordan curve itself is empty, and it must be
the boundary of the much smaller set in area of all points lying inside the curve.
5.4
A space ï¬lling curve, which is almost everywhere almost nowhere, and
vice versa

6
Nonmeasurable sets
6.1
Introduction
The nonmeasurable sets are very strange objects, which will be demonstrated in this chapter. We
start in Section 6.2, where we show the standard example of a non-measurable set. We are in this
construction forced to apply the dubious axiom of choice, which implies that it cannot be constructed
in a physical way.
For the same reason it is not possible to illustrate any nonmeasurable set on
a ï¬gure. In other words, nonmeasurable sets in practice only exist in the minds of mathematicians.
Whenever we consider a theory reï¬‚ecting something from the real world, then the functions are always
measurable. We mention here Hausdorï¬€â€™s paradox, which will be given in Section 6.4. It implies that
if one explicitly could construct (i.e. physically) nonmeasurable sets, then one would be able cut the
unit ball into 10 non-measurable pieces, where the four of them could be joined together to form
another full unit ball, just as the remaining six can be joined together to make up a full unit ball,
so we would have a means to double everything without making holes, i.e. without leaving out any
point. Practice from Physics shows that this is not possible in the real world.
6.2
A nonmeasurable set
We shall in this section â€œconstructâ€ a subset of R, which is not Lebesgue measurable. The doubtful
of this â€œconstructionâ€ is that it applies the plausible axiom of choice. However, even if the axiom

We shall later give some other weird
examples relying only on the axiom of choice, such like dividing the solid unit ball into jigsaw pieces,
which are then put together in a diï¬€erent way to form two solid unit balls without missing any point!
For the time being we shall assume the axiom of choice, cf. Section 6.3.

6.3
The axiom of choice
We have already pointed out that the questionable of the construction is the application of the axiom
of choice, when we from (6.1) choose an â€œexplicitâ€ xi âˆˆXi from the equivalence class Xi.
This
assumption is extremely plausible, but nevertheless it can neither be proved nor disproved within the
present system of axioms. We must either add this property or its negation as an extra axiom. We
formulate
The axiom of choice. Let M = âˆ…be any nonempty set, and let D(M) denote the system of all
subsets of M. There exists a function
u : D(M) \ {âˆ…} â†’M,
such that
u(A) âˆˆA
for every A âˆˆD(M) \ {âˆ…}.
In other words, there is a function u, which explicitly selects an element from every subset = âˆ…. This
is of course easy to construct, if M has a ï¬nite number n of elements, in which case D(M) \ {âˆ…}
consists of 2n âˆ’1 nonempty subsets. We just line them all up and choose an element in each of them,
deï¬ning in this way the function u.
If M, however, has inï¬nitely many elements, the method above does no longer apply. It can be proved
that D(N) has as many elements as the set R of all real numbers, so it is no longer possible to line
them all up in a table. It is even worse in the present case, where we constructed the nonmeasurable
set A given by (6.2). We chose M = R and then tacitly applied the axiom of choice on the large set
D(R) \ {âˆ…}. The set D(R) contains more elements then there are in R itself.
It can be proved that if the present system of mathematical axioms is consistent, it remains consistent
whether we add the axiom of choice or its negation. Axioms are characterized by this property.
To illustrate what is meant by this we consider another famous axiom, the parallel axiom in the
Euclidean geometry.
The parallel axiom. Two parallel straight lines are either coinciding, or they are never intersecting
each other.
Its negation is
The negated parallel axiom. There exist two diï¬€erent straight lines, which intersect each other,
The latter axiom is the basic axiom of the non-Euclidean geometry which was developped in the 19th
century.
Let us return to the axiom of choice in order to illustrate some of the problems involved with it. We
have already seen that it implies the existence of really nasty sets. We emphasize the word â€œexistenceâ€,
because there is no constructive way (e.g. an iterative method) to create such a nonmeasurable set.
They exist in the mind of some mathematicians, but never in the real world.
The axiom of choice has far more strange consequences. The most famous is
The well-ordering theorem. Every nonempty set M can be well-ordered.
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Nonmeasurable sets
182
This means that there exists an ordering relation â‰ºon M, such that every element a âˆˆM which is
not the last element, if such an element exists, has a uniquely determined successor b âˆˆM, i.e. there
is no element c âˆˆM, such that a â‰ºc â‰ºb, i.e. diï¬€erent from a and b and lying between them.
Clearly, (R, <) with the usual ordering relation < is not well-ordered, and it is not possible to get any
idea of how to construct a well-ordering (R, â‰º) of R.
In connection with the axiom of choice and the well-ordering theorem one usually also mentions
Zornâ€™s lemma, which mostly is used in mathematical proofs. We shall not formulate Zornâ€™s lemma,
only mention that the three mentioned statements are mathematically equivalent. Choose any of them
as an axiom, and the other two follows as theorems.
Outside the circles of mathematicians it is most convenient just to accept the axiom of choice, even if
it has some very strange consequences.
As a curiosity we mention that Alan Turing, who knew all the problems with the axiom of choice, in his
thesis, which has been reprinted in [4], tried to develop a theory for at least some of the nonmeasurable
sets. His ideas were to the authorâ€™s knowledge never followed up by others, though one never can tell.

6.4
The Hausdorï¬€paradox
In this section we shall assume the axiom of choice and show that it implies that the unit sphere in
R3 â€“ denoted S in the following â€“ can be divided into ten disjoint subsets, where four of these by only
translations and rotations in space can be put together, such that they form a unit sphere without
missing any point, while the remaining six subsets also can be put together, using only translations
and rotations in another jigsaw puzzle to form another unit sphere, again without missing any point.
If the pieces in this jigsaw puzzle were all measurable sets, this would of course be impossible, because
the measures then would add up in the expected way, and when we consider the areas, we would get
4Ï€ = 4Ï€ + 4Ï€, or put in another way, 1 = 1 + 1 = 2, which clearly is nonsense. Here we claim that it
is possible to double the area using only isometries by allowing non-measurable sets in the division of
S. This is what is called Hausdorï¬€â€™s paradox.
The paradox is easily derived from a result, ï¬rst proved by Hausdorï¬€[25] in 1914. We shall here more
or less follow the proof given by Sierpinski [50]. This proof can also be found in Sierpinski, Klein,
Runge & Dickson [51].
It is remarkable that the proof only requires a little knowledge of elementary Group Theory, some
knowledge of elementary Geometry, and Calculus at high school level. It is, however, very long, and
the reader must be painstakingly accurate in the arguments. The axiom of choice, cf. Section 6.3,
is used only once in the proof in an apparently very innocent way, and we nevertheless obtain this
paradox.
We shall ï¬rst introduce some terminology.
Deï¬nition 6.1 A map T : R3 â†’R3 is called an isometry, if it preserves Euclidean distances, i.e.
T x âˆ’T y2 = x âˆ’y2,
for all x, y âˆˆR3.
It should be well-known that an isometry T in R3 is either a translation, or a rotation, or a reï¬‚ection,
or a composition of the above mentioned simple isometries. We shall not need the reï¬‚ections in the
following, and the translations are only used at the very end of the construction to put the jigsaw
pieces together, so the proof will mostly be applications of rotations and their compositions.
Deï¬nition 6.2 Two subsets A and B of R3 are said to be congruent, or isometric, if there exists an
isometry T : R3 â†’R3, such that B = T (A). When A and B are isometric, we write A âˆ¼= B.
Obviously the two deï¬nitions above apply in any Euclidean space. In this section we shall only use
them in R3.
Deï¬nition 6.3 Two subsets A and B of R3 are called n-equivalent,, and we write A =n B, if there
exist 2n subsets A1, . . . , An and B1, . . . , Bn, such that
1) A = n
j=1 Aj and B = n
j=1 Bj.
2) Each of the two systems {Aj | j = 1, . . . , n} and {Bj | j = 1, . . . , n} consists of mutually disjoint
sets.
3) Aj âˆ¼= Bj for all j = 1, . . . , n.

Recall the following theorem from elementary Geometry.
Theorem 6.3 If a body is transformed isometrically in R3, such that one of its points is a ï¬xpoint,

We shall apply Hausdorï¬€â€™s paradox, cf. Section 6.4, to derive
Banach-Tarskiâ€™s paradox. If E1 and E2 are two bounded sets in R3, both with interior points, i.e.
Eâ—¦

of gold of the double size. Such a procedure would of course be devastating for the worldâ€™s economy,

7.1
Introduction
In this chapter we collect some examples, which do not belong to the classiï¬cation of any of the other
chapters. It is on purpose that we have put it before Chapter 8, which must be considered to be the
most diï¬ƒcult chapter in this book. Here we ï¬rst consider the method of induction in Section 7.2.
This section should not be taken too seriously, because in practice most applications of the method
of induction are straight forward. Then in the following Section 7.3 we play a little with series. The
theory becomes more serious in Section 7.4, in which we organise the set of irrational numbers R \ Q
as a complete set without changing the topology inherited from R. Finally, we show in Section 3.8 an
alternative way of solving the heat equation, where the method applied here is not commonly known
in the literature.
7.2
The method of induction
We shall in this section ï¬rst give an anecdote, in which a mathematician, a physicist and an engineer
are making fools of themselves by applying the method of proof by induction in a wrong way. Then
follows an example, in which the standard proof of induction is successfully demonstrated, and then
ï¬nally we demonstrate that there may even in the standard method of proof by induction be some
unexpected pitfalls.
7.2.1
An anecdote
There are lots of anecdotes describing the diï¬€erence in thinking of mathematicians, physicists and
engineers. They are all more or less very silly, and yet they often hit the head of the nail, concerning
the characteristics of these three scientiï¬c tribes, where the author himself feels that he is a part of
all three of them.
A mathematician, a physicist and an engineer are sitting together discussing how to â€œproveâ€ that all
odd numbers, except for 1, are primes.
First the mathematician says: â€œThis is easy, because 3 is a prime, 5 is a prime and 7 is a prime, and
then the claim follows by induction!â€
The physicist is not convinced. â€œNo, no!â€, he objects. â€œYou must reason in the following way: 3 is
a prime, 5 is a prime, 7 is a prime, 9 is an experimental error, 11 is a prime, 13 is a prime, and the
claim follows!â€
â€œWell, well, well!â€, says the engineer. â€œIt is much easier to prove, because 3 is a prime, 5 is a prime,
7 is a prime, 9 is a prime, 11 is a prime, etc..â€
The usual reaction is that mathematicians cannot see that this is a joke. Physicists are in general
really amused, while engineers are embarrassed.
All three proofs are wrong, including the mathematicianâ€™s application of the proof by induction. We
shall in the next section in a simple example give a standard method of the proof by inductions, and
ï¬nally in Section 7.2.3 follow this standard procedure in a case, where it cannot be applied, due to a
hidden pitfall.

7.2.3
Wrong application of the principle of induction
We shall in this section by induction â€œproveâ€ that given any bundle Ln of n mutually nonparallel
straight lines in the plane, they will all pass through the same point in the plane. This claim is
obviously wrong.
The â€œproofâ€ goes as follows. The claim is trivial for n = 1, because then we only have one line. For
n = 2 the bundle L2 contains two straight lines which are not parallel. They must therefore intersect
each other at a point, and the claim is also true for n = 2.
Then assume that given any bundle Ln, consisting of n mutually nonparallel straight lines from Ln
these will all intersect each other at a point x, say. This is at least true for n = 1 and n = 2.
Consider any such bundle Ln+1 of n+ 1 mutually nonparallel straight lines. If we remove one of these
lines, we reduce the situation to a bundle Ln consisting of n lines. By assumption, they intersect each
other at a point x. Then we remove another line to get another bundle Lâ€²
n, consisting of n lines. By
assumption they must also intersect at a point y. Then the bundle Lnâˆ’1 in which we have removed
both lines above, must intersect each other at both x and y. Since the lines of Lnâˆ’1 are mutually
nonparallel, this is only possible if x = y, and the claim â€œfollowsâ€ by induction.
This is an example of what happens, if one argues too formally and jumps to conclusions. The proof
follows the usual scheme of a proof by induction. Assuming that some claim is right for some n, we
prove that it also holds for the successor n + 1. Furthermore, we checked that the claim was true for
both n = 1 and n = 2, so it should follow by this bootstrap method. However, it does not hold for
n = 3.
Figure 7.1: A bundle L3 consisting of three mutually nonparallel straight lines in the plane and not
passing through a common point.
If we in the bundle L3 of Figure 7.1 remove â„“1, the intersection point is x. If we instead remove â„“2,
then the intersection point is y. There is only one line in L3 \ {â„“1, â„“2} = {â„“3}, and it surely passes
through both x and y = x. We see that the induction step from n = 2 to n = 2 + 1 = 3 does not
apply.

7.4
The irrational numbers organised as a complete metric space without
changing the subspace topology inherited from R
This pathological example is mostly of interest for mathematicians. Let us ï¬rst recall some topological
notions.
Let M = âˆ…be a nonempty set. A metric on M is a map d : M Ã— M â†’R, which fulï¬ls the following
four conditions:
m1) The distance between two points x, y âˆˆM is always nonnegative, i.e.
d(x, y) â‰¥0
for all x, y âˆˆM.
m2) The distance between two points x, y âˆˆM is 0, if and only if the points are identical, x = y, i.e.
d(x, y) = 0
if and only if
x = y.
m3) The distance from x to y is the same as the distance from y to x, i.e.
d(x, y) = d(y, x)
for all x, y âˆˆM.
m4) The triangle inequality. The distance between two points x and y is smaller than or equal to the
distance from x to z plus the distance from z to y, i.e.
d(x, y) â‰¤d(x, z) + d(z, y),
i.e. a detour is always longer (or at least equal to) than the straight way.
When d is a metric on M, we call (M, d) a metric space.
We deï¬ne the open ball of centre x âˆˆM and radius r > 0 in the metric space (M, d) as the set
Bd(x, r) := {y âˆˆM | d(x, y) < r}.
Similarly,
Bd[x, r] := {y âˆˆM | d(x, y) â‰¤r}
denotes the closed ball of centre x âˆˆM and radius r > 0 in the metric d.
Assume that d1 and d2 are two metrics on M. We say that the two metric spaces (M, d1) and (M, d2)
â€“ or just the metrics d1 and d2 â€“ are equivalent, if the following two conditions are fulï¬lled.
e1) For every x âˆˆM and every r > 0 there exists an R > 0, such that
Bd1(x, R) âŠ†Bd2(x, r).
e2) For every x âˆˆM and every r > 0 there exists an R > 0, such that
Bd2(x, R) âŠ†Bd1(x, r).
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

MY HORROR CHAMBER
212
Miscellaneous
212
In other words, every ball in the d2-metric contains a ball in the d1-metric, and vice versa.
A sequence (xn) of points from the metric space (M, d) is called convergent in the metric d, if there
exists a point x âˆˆM, such that for any given Îµ > 0 there is a constant N âˆˆN, such that
d (xn, x) < Îµ
for all n â‰¥N.
In other words, the elements of (xn) eventually belong to the open ball Bd(x, Îµ)
When (xn) is convergent in the metric d, then the point x is uniquely determined. In fact, if the
elements of (xn) eventually belong to both Bd(x, Îµ) and Bd(y, Îµ), and x = y, then we obtain a
contradiction by choosing Îµ < d(x, y)/2, because then Bd(x, Îµ) âˆ©Bd(y, Îµ) = âˆ…. Hence, x is uniquely
determined, when (xn) is convergent in (M, d), and we call x the limit of (xn), and we write
lim
nâ†’+âˆxn = x.
A sequence (xn) from (M, d) is called a Cauchy sequence (with respect to the metric d), if for every
Îµ > 0 there is an N âˆˆN, such that
d(xm, xn) < Îµ,
whenever m, n â‰¥N.
It is easy to prove that every convergent sequence is also a Cauchy sequence. In fact, choose N âˆˆN,
such that
d(xm, x) < Îµ
2
and
d(xn, x) < Îµ

On the other hand, a Cauchy sequence is not always convergent. An example is given in (Q, | Â· |) by
x1 = 3;
x2 = 3.1;
x3 = 3.14;
x4 = 3.141;
x5 = 3.1415;
x6 = 3.14159;
. . . ,
where we approximate Ï€ by the sequence of its decimal fractions. Since the limit Ï€ /âˆˆQ, this sequence
is not convergent in (Q, |Â·|), though it is in the larger space (R, |Â·|), so it is clearly a Cauchy sequence.
If a metric space (M, d) has the property that every Cauchy sequence is also convergent with a limit
in M, then we call (M, d) a complete metric space.
The prototype of a complete space is the space of all real numbers, (R; | Â· |) in the usual metric,
d(x, y) := |x âˆ’y|. In general we deï¬ne the usual metric on a subset A âŠ†R by
d(x, y) := |x âˆ’y|
for x, y âˆˆA,
A âŠ†R,
and we denote the metric space by (A, | Â· |).
As mentioned above, (R, | Â· |) is complete, but neither (Q, | Â· |) nor (R \ Q, | Â· |) are complete. In the
latter case, just consider the sequence (2âˆ’nÏ€) from the set of irrational numbers R\Q. It is obviously
a Cauchy sequence, which in the larger space (R, | Â· |) of formally the same metric converges towards
0 /âˆˆR \ Q, and we know already that the limit is unique, when it exists.
After all these preparations the following theorem should indeed be surprising.

1) (R \ Q, d) and (R \ Q, | Â· |) are equivalent metric spaces.

Miscellaneous
Although this result is nice, it is for the time being not the most useful one. The reason is that at
present the problem is usually a diï¬€erent one, namely given a set of data, how can we approximate
this set of data by linear combinations of solutions of the type (7.17), where we tacitly have included
the diï¬€usion coeï¬ƒcient in the problem (7.8)?
The ï¬rst attempt in that direction was made by
Collepardi [12], [13] and [14], from 1970 and the following years. He suggested to approximate a set
of measurements by the complementary error function, i.e. by
(7.18)
c0 erfc

a
x
âˆš
4t

= c0 Î¨0

a
x
âˆš
4t

,
with the two unknown constants a and c0.
By using the statistical method of least squares to ï¬x the two unknown constants a and c0, he obtained
a lot of excellent results, which were not known before, so this was really a big step forward. In the
1980s, however, one discovered some examples where the Collepardi method did not apply very well.
This was why there was a need of a better model.
In line with the above, which was not known before 1970, the next approach would be to model with
(7.19)
c0 Î¨

a
x
âˆš
4t

+ c1 t Î¨1

a
x
âˆš
4t

with three unknowns a, c0 and c2 and then use the method of the least square approximation. Neither
of the two approximations are fully correct. In (7.18) we tacitly approximate the boundary value
ËœÏ•(t) with a constant c0, and in the second case (7.19) we approximate the boundary value ËœÏ•(t) with
the aï¬ƒne map c0 + c1t. Since the latter one includes the former one by choosing c1 = 0, we must
necessarily get a better approximation by using (7.19) instead of (7.18).
Notice that if the same set of measurements is modelled by both (7.18) and (7.19), the two constants
a and c1 are not the same in the two cases, when c1 = 0.
One should also notice that the approximation (7.18) is always decreasing in x, when t is ï¬xed,
while (7.19) in some cases for ï¬xed t is ï¬rst increasing in x to some maximum, before it decreases.
This is apparently against common sense, although it was predicted in 1995.
Recently, in 2015,
this phenomenon was actually observed in some data measured at the Swedish research station at
TrÂ¨aslÂ¨ovslÂ¨age on the Swedish west coast. Here slaps of concrete of diï¬€erent composition had been
exposed to salty water for 20 years. In the measurements they discovered that in some cases the
concentration of chloride ions reached a maximum at some distance from the surface. This can never
be modelled by the complementary error function solution alone. But it can by the more general
model of (7.19).
It is obvious that we can extend this method by including more terms of the special structure in the
approximation. Each time we add a new unknown constant, which should be found by the method of
least squares. There is no need here to go further into this theory.

8
Measures in Rn and in inï¬nite dimensional Hilbert space
8.1
Introduction
We shall in Section 8.2 consider the geometry and measure of balls in Rn for large n. This theory is the
stepping stone for Section 8.3, in which we consider a Gaussian measure on an inï¬nitely dimensional
separable Hilbert space, for which no diï¬€erentiation theorem exists with respect to the systems of
small balls of contracting radii (and ï¬xed centre). We shall use this result in Section 8.4 to derive an
unexpected positivity principle in Hilbert space.
8.2
Surface measure and volume measure in Rn
Most people have a vague sense of that the Euclidean geometry of Rn resembles the ordinary geometry
of the usual space R3. However, it should not be a surprise that we also here may ï¬nd some unexpected
results. We shall here only deal with two closely connected results, none of which can be called obvious.
We shall in the following also vary the dimension n âˆˆN, so let us ï¬rst ï¬x the notation,

Once Theorem 8.2 has been proved, it is not hard to obtain other strange examples. We here include
